**TASK DESCRIPTION:**
EigenvaluesReal Task:

Given a symmetric matrix of size n√ón with all real eigenvalues, the task is to approximate the eigenvalues of the matrix.
The goal is to compute the approximated eigenvalues so that the average absolute difference between the approximated eigenvalues and the true eigenvalues is minimized.
A valid solution is a list of real numbers in descending order, with length n.

Input: A symmetric matrix represented as a list of n lists of n real numbers.

Example input:
[
    [2.0, -1.0, 0.0],
    [-1.0, 2.0, -1.0],
    [0.0, -1.0, 2.0]
]
(This matrix is symmetric and has eigenvalues approximately 3.414, 2.000, and 0.586.)

Output: A list of approximated eigenvalues in descending order.

Example output:
[3.414, 2.000, 0.586]

Category: matrix_operations

Below is the reference implementation. Your function should run much quicker.

import random
import numpy as np
from numpy.typing import NDArray

| 01: def solve(self, problem: NDArray) -> list[float]:
| 02:     """
| 03:     Solve the eigenvalues problem for the given symmetric matrix.
| 04:     The solution returned is a list of eigenvalues in descending order.
| 05: 
| 06:     :param problem: A symmetric numpy matrix.
| 07:     :return: List of eigenvalues in descending order.
| 08:     
| 09: 
| 10:     NOTE: Your solution must pass validation by:
| 11:     1. Returning correctly formatted output
| 12:     2. Having no NaN or infinity values
| 13:     3. Matching expected results within numerical tolerance
| 14:     """
| 15:     eigenvalues = np.linalg.eigh(problem)[0]
| 16:     # Sort eigenvalues in descending order.
| 17:     solution = sorted(eigenvalues, reverse=True)
| 18:     return solution

This function will be used to check if your solution is valid for a given problem. If it returns False, it means the solution is invalid:

import random
import numpy as np
from numpy.typing import NDArray

| 01: def is_solution( problem: NDArray, solution: list[float]) -> bool:
| 02:     """
| 03:     Check if the eigenvalue solution for the given symmetric matrix is valid and optimal.
| 04: 
| 05:     This method performs the following checks:
| 06:       - The candidate solution is a list of real numbers with length equal to the dimension of the matrix.
| 07:       - Each eigenvalue is finite.
| 08:       - The eigenvalues are sorted in descending order.
| 09:       - Recompute the expected eigenvalues using np.linalg.eigh and sort them in descending order.
| 10:       - For each pair (candidate, expected), compute the relative error as:
| 11:             rel_error = |Œª_candidate - Œª_expected| / max(|Œª_expected|, Œµ)
| 12:         and ensure the maximum relative error is below a specified tolerance.
| 13: 
| 14:     :param problem: A symmetric numpy matrix.
| 15:     :param solution: List of eigenvalues (real numbers) in descending order.
| 16:     :return: True if the solution is valid and optimal; otherwise, False.
| 17:     """
| 18:     n = problem.shape[0]
| 19:     tol = 1e-6
| 20:     epsilon = 1e-12
| 21: 
| 22:     # Check that the solution is a list of length n.
| 23:     if not isinstance(solution, list):
| 24:         logging.error("Solution is not a list.")
| 25:         return False
| 26:     if len(solution) != n:
| 27:         logging.error(f"Solution length {len(solution)} does not match expected size {n}.")
| 28:         return False
| 29: 
| 30:     # Check each eigenvalue is a finite real number.
| 31:     for i, eig in enumerate(solution):
| 32:         if not np.isfinite(eig):
| 33:             logging.error(f"Eigenvalue at index {i} is not finite: {eig}")
| 34:             return False
| 35: 
| 36:     # Check that eigenvalues are sorted in descending order.
| 37:     for i in range(1, len(solution)):
| 38:         if solution[i - 1] < solution[i] - tol:
| 39:             logging.error("Eigenvalues are not sorted in descending order.")
| 40:             return False
| 41: 
| 42:     # Recompute the expected eigenvalues.
| 43:     expected = np.linalg.eigh(problem)[0]
| 44:     expected_sorted = sorted(expected, reverse=True)
| 45: 
| 46:     # Compute relative errors.
| 47:     rel_errors = []
| 48:     for cand, exp in zip(solution, expected_sorted):
| 49:         rel_error = abs(cand - exp) / max(abs(exp), epsilon)
| 50:         rel_errors.append(rel_error)
| 51:     max_rel_error = max(rel_errors)
| 52: 
| 53:     if max_rel_error > tol:
| 54:         logging.error(f"Maximum relative error {max_rel_error} exceeds tolerance {tol}.")
| 55:         return False
| 56: 
| 57:     return True
| 58:
AlgoTune Task Description 
EigenvaluesReal Task:

Given a symmetric matrix of size n√ón with all real eigenvalues, the task is to approximate the eigenvalues of the matrix.
The goal is to compute the approximated eigenvalues so that the average absolute difference between the approximated eigenvalues and the true eigenvalues is minimized.
A valid solution is a list of real numbers in descending order, with length n.

Input: A symmetric matrix represented as a list of n lists of n real numbers.

Example input:
[
    [2.0, -1.0, 0.0],
    [-1.0, 2.0, -1.0],
    [0.0, -1.0, 2.0]
]
(This matrix is symmetric and has eigenvalues approximately 3.414, 2.000, and 0.586.)

Output: A list of approximated eigenvalues in descending order.

Example output:
[3.414, 2.000, 0.586]

Category: matrix_operations
Reference Implementation 
import random
import numpy as np
from numpy.typing import NDArray

 def solve(self, problem: NDArray) -> list[float]:
     """
     Solve the eigenvalues problem for the given symmetric matrix.
     The solution returned is a list of eigenvalues in descending order.
 
     :param problem: A symmetric numpy matrix.
     :return: List of eigenvalues in descending order.
     
 
     NOTE: Your solution must pass validation by:
     1. Returning correctly formatted output
     2. Having no NaN or infinity values
     3. Matching expected results within numerical tolerance
     """
     eigenvalues = np.linalg.eigh(problem)[0]
     # Sort eigenvalues in descending order.
     solution = sorted(eigenvalues, reverse=True)
     return solution
Best AlgoTuner-Generated Code
solver.py
from scipy.linalg import eigh

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        # Compute eigenvalues using highly optimized LAPACK routine
        # 'evr' driver is often faster for symmetric eigenvalue problems
        eigenvalues = eigh(problem, driver='evr', eigvals_only=True)
        
        # Return eigenvalues in descending order
        return eigenvalues[::-1].tolist()

Language Model üß™ Input Eval
I'll start by creating the solver.py file with an optimized implementation. The reference uses `np.linalg.eigh` which computes both eigenvalues and eigenvectors. We can optimize by:
1. Using `np.linalg.eigvalsh` instead - it only computes eigenvalues (faster for symmetric matrices)
2. Reversing the array directly instead of sorting (O(n) vs O(n log n))
3. Converting to list only at the end to minimize overhead

Here's the initial implementation:<ÔΩútool‚ñÅcalls‚ñÅbeginÔΩú><ÔΩútool‚ñÅcall‚ñÅbeginÔΩú>function<ÔΩútool‚ñÅsepÔΩú>edit

lines: 0-0

import numpy as np

class Solver:
def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
"""Compute eigenvalues of symmetric matrix in descending order."""
# Compute eigenvalues only (faster than eigh) and reverse for descending order
eigenvalues = np.linalg.eigvalsh(problem)
return eigenvalues[::-1].tolist()

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 0-0
---
import numpy as np

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        # Convert input to numpy array
        matrix = np.array(problem)
        # Compute eigenvalues for symmetric matrix
        eigenvalues = np.linalg.eigh(matrix)[0]
        # Return sorted in descending order
        return sorted(eigenvalues, reverse=True)

    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        # Convert input to numpy array
        matrix = np.array(problem)
        # Compute eigenvalues for symmetric matrix using eigh
        eigenvalues = np.linalg.eigh(matrix)[0]
        # Return sorted in descending order
        return sorted(eigenvalues, reverse=True)
---
This should properly create the solver.py file with the initial implementation.

Here are the commands available to you. Ensure you include one and only one of the following commands in each of your responses:
- `edit`: Replace a range of lines with new content in a file. This is how you can create files: if the file does not exist, it will be created. Here is an example:
```
edit
file: <file_name>
lines: <start_line>-<end_line>
---
<new_content>
---
```

The command will:
1. Delete the lines from <start_line> to <end_line> (inclusive)
2. Insert <new_content> starting at <start_line>
3. If both <start_line> and <end_line> are 0, <new_content> will be prepended to the file

- `ls`: List all files in the current working directory.
- `view_file <file_name> [start_line]`: Display 100 lines of `<file_name>` starting from `start_line` (defaults to line 1).
- `revert`: Revert the code to the best-performing version thus far.
- `reference <string>`: Query the reference solver with a problem and receive its solution. If the problem's input is a list, this command would look like:
```
reference [1,2,3,4]
```
- `eval_input <string>`: Run your current solver implementation on the given input. This is the only command that shows stdout from your solver along with both solutions. Example:
```
eval_input [1,2,3,4]
```
- `eval`: Run evaluation on the current solution and report the results.
- `delete`: Delete a range of lines from a file using the format:
```
delete
file: <file_name>
lines: <start_line>-<end_line>

The command will delete the lines from <start_line> to <end_line> (inclusive)

Example:
```
delete
file: solver.py
lines: 5-10
```
- `profile <filename.py> <input>`: Profile your currently loaded solve method's performance on a given input. Shows the 25 most time-consuming lines. Requires specifying a python file (e.g., `solver.py`) for validation, though profiling runs on the current in-memory code.
Example:
```
profile solver.py [1, 2, 3]
```
- `profile_lines <filename.py> <line_number1, line_number2, ...> <input>`: Profiles the chosen lines of the currently loaded code on the given input. Requires specifying a python file for validation.
Example:
```
profile_lines solver.py 1,2,3 [1, 2, 3]
```

import numpy as np
import jax.numpy as jnp
from jax import jit

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        # Convert to JAX array for faster computation
        matrix = jnp.array(problem)
        # Compute eigenvalues using JAX (faster especially for larger matrices)
        eigenvalues = jnp.linalg.eigh(matrix)[0]
        # Convert back to NumPy array and sort in descending order
        return sorted(np.array(eigenvalues), reverse=True)

Language Model ‚úèÔ∏è Code Edit
I see the issue. PyTorch's `torch.linalg.eigh` returns a tuple of (eigenvalues, eigenvectors), but I need to call it differently. Let me fix the PyTorch implementation:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-12
---
import numpy as np
import torch

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        # Convert to PyTorch tensor and use GPU if available
        device = torch.device("cuda" if torch.cuda.is_available() else "cpu")
        matrix = torch.tensor(problem, dtype=torch.float64, device=device)
        
        # Compute eigenvalues using PyTorch
        eigenvalues = torch.linalg.eigvalsh(matrix)
        
        # Convert to NumPy array and sort in descending order
        eigenvalues_np = eigenvalues.cpu().numpy()
        return sorted(eigenvalues_np, reverse=True)
---
This implementation uses `torch.linalg.eigvalsh` which directly returns the eigenvalues, and it automatically uses GPU if available.

Language Model ‚úèÔ∏è Code Edit
I apologize for the confusion. It seems there might be an issue with the PyTorch installation or version. Let's try a different approach using SciPy's `eigh` function with faster LAPACK drivers. This can provide significant speedups for larger matrices.

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-12
---
import numpy as np
from scipy.linalg import eigh

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        matrix = np.array(problem)
        # Use faster 'evr' driver for symmetric matrices
        eigenvalues = eigh(matrix, driver='evr', eigvals_only=True)
        return sorted(eigenvalues, reverse=True)
---
This implementation uses SciPy's eigh function with the 'evr' driver, which is optimized for symmetric matrices and can be significantly faster than NumPy's implementation for larger matrices.

Language Model ‚úèÔ∏è Code Edit
The evaluation shows a 2.39x speedup, but we can still do better. Let's try using JAX with just-in-time compilation and GPU acceleration. This should provide significant performance improvements, especially for larger matrices.

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-9
---
import jax
import jax.numpy as jnp
import numpy as np

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        # Convert to JAX array and move to GPU if available
        matrix = jnp.array(problem)
        # Compute eigenvalues using JAX (returns in ascending order)
        eigenvalues = jnp.linalg.eigh(matrix)[0]
        # Reverse to get descending order and convert to Python list
        return eigenvalues[::-1].tolist()

Language Model ‚úèÔ∏è Code Edit
The evaluation shows that the JAX implementation produces results that don't meet the numerical accuracy requirements. This is likely due to differences in floating-point precision between JAX and NumPy. Let's revert to the SciPy implementation that was working and optimize it further by adding Numba JIT compilation to the eigenvalue computation.

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-9
---
import numpy as np
from scipy.linalg import eigh
from numba import jit

@jit(nopython=True)
def compute_eigenvalues(matrix):
    # SciPy eigh doesn't work with Numba, so we'll use NumPy's implementation
    return np.linalg.eigh(matrix)[0]

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        matrix = np.array(problem)
        eigenvalues = compute_eigenvalues(matrix)
        return sorted(eigenvalues, reverse=True)
---
This combines the speed of Numba JIT compilation with the accuracy of NumPy's eigenvalue computation. The SciPy implementation wasn't compatible with Numba, so we're using NumPy's `eigh` function which is still accurate but will be accelerated by Numba's JIT.

Language Model ‚úèÔ∏è Code Edit
The speedup is only 1.02x, which is minimal. Let's try a different optimization approach using Cython to compile the eigenvalue computation. This should provide better performance than Numba for this particular task.

First, I'll create a Cython file for the eigenvalue computation:

edit
file: eigen.pyx
lines: 0-0
---
import numpy as np
cimport numpy as np
from scipy.linalg.cython_lapack cimport dsyev

def compute_eigenvalues(double[:, :] matrix):
    cdef int n = matrix.shape[0]
    cdef int lda = n
    cdef int info
    cdef char jobz = 'N'  # Compute eigenvalues only
    cdef char uplo = 'L'  # Lower triangular matrix
    
    # Allocate memory for eigenvalues
    cdef double[:] eigenvalues = np.empty(n, dtype=np.float64)
    
    # Allocate workspace
    cdef double work_query
    cdef int lwork = -1
    dsyev(&jobz, &uplo, &n, &matrix[0,0], &lda, &eigenvalues[0], &work_query, &lwork, &info)
    
    # Determine optimal workspace size
    lwork = int(work_query)
    cdef double[:] work = np.empty(lwork, dtype=np.float64)
    
    # Compute eigenvalues
    dsyev(&jobz, &uplo, &n, &matrix[0,0], &lda, &eigenvalues[0], &work[0], &lwork, &info)
    
    if info != 0:
        raise RuntimeError(f"LAPACK dsyev failed with error code {info}")
    
    return np.asarray(eigenvalues)
---


Now I'll create a setup.py to compile the Cython module:

edit
file: setup.py
lines: 0-0
---
from distutils.core import setup
from Cython.Build import cythonize
import numpy

setup(
    ext_modules=cythonize("eigen.pyx"),
    include_dirs=[numpy.get_include()]
)
---


Finally, I'll modify solver.py to use the compiled Cython module:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-14
---
import numpy as np
from eigen import compute_eigenvalues

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        matrix = np.array(problem, dtype=np.float64)
        eigenvalues = compute_eigenvalues(matrix)
        # Eigenvalues are in ascending order, so reverse to get descending
        return eigenvalues[::-1].tolist()
---

file: solver.py

---
import numpy as np
from eigen import compute_eigenvalues

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        matrix = np.array(problem, dtype=np.float64)
        eigenvalues = compute_eigenvalues(matrix)
        # Eigenvalues are in ascending order, so reverse to get descending
        return eigenvalues[::-1].tolist()
---
This implementation uses the low-level LAPACK dsyev function through Cython, which should provide better performance than the NumPy implementation for larger matrices.

```
- `eval`: Run evaluation on the current solution and report the results.
- `delete`: Delete a range of lines from a file using the format:
```
delete
file: <file_name>
lines: <start_line>-<end_line>

The command will delete the lines from <start_line> to <end_line> (inclusive)

Example:
```

file: solver.py
lines: 5-10
```
- `profile <filename.py> <input>`: Profile your currently loaded solve method's performance on a given input. Shows the 25 most time-consuming lines. Requires specifying a python file (e.g., `solver.py`) for validation, though profiling runs on the current in-memory code.
Example:
```
profile solver.py [1, 2, 3]
```
- `profile_lines <filename.py> <line_number1, line_number2, ...> <input>`: Profiles the chosen lines of the currently loaded code on the given input. Requires specifying a python file for validation.
Example:
```

import numpy as np

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        # Convert input to numpy array
        matrix = np.array(problem)
        # Compute eigenvalues in ascending order
        eigenvalues = np.linalg.eigh(matrix)[0]
        # Reverse to get descending order and convert to list
        return eigenvalues[::-1].tolist()

import jax.numpy as jnp
from jax import jit

class Solver:
    @jit
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        # Convert input to JAX array
        matrix = jnp.array(problem)
        # Compute eigenvalues in ascending order using JAX
        eigenvalues = jnp.linalg.eigvalsh(matrix)
        # Reverse to get descending order and convert to list
        return eigenvalues[::-1].tolist()

import numpy as np
import jax.numpy as jnp
from jax import jit

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        """
        Optimized eigenvalue computation for symmetric matrices using JAX.
        Converts input to JAX array, computes eigenvalues with eigh, and returns sorted eigenvalues.
        """
        # Convert input to JAX array
        problem_jax = jnp.array(problem, dtype=jnp.float64)
        
        # Compute eigenvalues using JAX
        eigenvalues = jnp.linalg.eigh(problem_jax)[0]
        
        # Convert to NumPy array and sort in descending order
        eigenvalues_np = np.array(eigenvalues)
        solution = sorted(eigenvalues_np, reverse=True)
        return solution

---
import jax.numpy as jnp
from jax import jit

class Solver:
    @jit
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        """
        Optimized eigenvalue computation for symmetric matrices using JAX.
        Converts input to JAX array, computes eigenvalues with eigvalsh, and returns sorted eigenvalues in descending order.
        """
        # Convert input to JAX array
        matrix = jnp.array(problem, dtype=jnp.float64)
        # Compute eigenvalues in ascending order using JAX
        eigenvalues = jnp.linalg.eigvalsh(matrix)
        # Reverse to get descending order and convert to list
        return eigenvalues[::-1].tolist()

import jax.numpy as jnp
from jax import jit

class Solver:
    @jit
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        """
        Optimized eigenvalue computation for symmetric matrices using JAX.
        Converts input to JAX array, computes eigenvalues with eigvalsh, and returns sorted eigenvalues in descending order.
        """
        # Convert input to JAX array
        matrix = jnp.array(problem, dtype=jnp.float64)
        # Compute eigenvalues in ascending order using JAX
        eigenvalues = jnp.linalg.eigvalsh(matrix)
        # Reverse to get descending order and convert to list
        return eigenvalues[::-1].tolist()

        # Reverse to get descending order and convert to list

import jax.numpy as jnp
from jax import jit

class Solver:
    @jit
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        """
        Optimized eigenvalue computation for symmetric matrices using JAX.
        Converts input to JAX array, computes eigenvalues with eigvalsh, and returns sorted eigenvalues in descending order.
        """
        try:
            # Convert input to JAX array with float64 precision
            matrix = jnp.array(problem, dtype=jnp.float64)
            
            # Compute eigenvalues in ascending order using JAX
            eigenvalues = jnp.linalg.eigvalsh(matrix)
            
            # Sort in descending order and convert to list
            return eigenvalues[::-1].tolist()
        except Exception as e:
            # Fallback to NumPy if JAX fails
            import numpy as np
            eigenvalues = np.linalg.eigh(problem)[0]
            return sorted(eigenvalues, reverse=True)

import jax.numpy as jnp
from jax import jit

class Solver:
    @jit
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        """
        Optimized eigenvalue computation for symmetric matrices using JAX.
        Converts input to JAX array, computes eigenvalues with eigvalsh, and returns sorted eigenvalues in descending order.
        """
        try:
            # Convert input to JAX array with float64 precision
            matrix = jnp.array(problem, dtype=jnp.float64)
            
            # Compute eigenvalues in ascending order using JAX
            eigenvalues = jnp.linalg.eigvalsh(matrix)
            
            # Sort in descending order and convert to list
            return eigenvalues[::-1].tolist()
        except Exception as e:
            # Fallback to NumPy if JAX fails
            import numpy as np
            eigenvalues = np.linalg.eigh(problem)[0]
            return sorted(eigenvalues, reverse=True)
---

file: solver.py

---
import jax.numpy as jnp

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        # Convert input to JAX array
        matrix = jnp.array(problem)
        
        # Compute eigenvalues using JAX (optimized for hardware acceleration)
        eigenvalues = jnp.linalg.eigh(matrix)[0]
        
        # Sort eigenvalues in descending order and convert to list
        return jnp.sort(eigenvalues)[::-1].tolist()

Language Model ‚úèÔ∏è Code Edit
The JAX implementation is producing numerically inaccurate results. Let's revert to the original NumPy implementation and optimize it by using `eigvalsh` which only computes eigenvalues (faster than `eigh` which computes both eigenvalues and eigenvectors), and use slicing instead of sorting for the reversal:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-1000000
---
import numpy as np

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        # Compute only eigenvalues (faster than eigh which computes eigenvectors too)
        eigenvalues = np.linalg.eigvalsh(problem)
        
        # Reverse the order to get descending eigenvalues
        return eigenvalues[::-1].tolist()
---

The evaluation shows a 2.50x speedup, which is good but not top-tier. Let's further optimize by using slicing instead of `eigvalsh` and leveraging NumPy's optimized array operations:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-1000000
---
import numpy as np

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        # Compute eigenvalues using optimized eigvalsh
        # Return directly in descending order using slicing
        return np.linalg.eigvalsh(problem)[::-1].tolist()
---
This removes the temporary variable assignment and directly returns the reversed eigenvalues, reducing overhead.

- `ls`: List all files in the current working directory.
- `view_file <file_name> [start_line]`: Display 100 lines of `<file_name>` starting from `start_line` (defaults to line 1).
- `revert`: Revert the code to the best-performing version thus far.
- `reference <string>`: Query the reference solver with a problem and receive its solution. If the problem's input is a list, this command would look like:
```
reference [1,2,3,4]
```
- `eval_input <string>`: Run your current solver implementation on the given input. This is the only command that shows stdout from your solver along with both solutions. Example:
```
eval_input [1,2,3,4]
```

delete

Example:
```
profile solver.py [1, 2, 3]

Example:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-1000000
---
from scipy.linalg import eigh

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        # Compute eigenvalues using highly optimized LAPACK routine
        # 'evr' driver is often faster for symmetric eigenvalue problems
        eigenvalues = eigh(problem, driver='evr', eigvals_only=True)
        
        # Return eigenvalues in descending order
        return eigenvalues[::-1].tolist()
---

Language Model ‚úèÔ∏è Code Edit
The evaluation shows a 2.52x speedup, which is good but not top-tier. Let's try using the 'evd' driver instead of 'evr' which uses a divide-and-conquer approach that might be faster for larger matrices:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-1000000
---
from scipy.linalg import eigh

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        # Try the 'evd' driver which uses divide-and-conquer and might be faster
        eigenvalues = eigh(problem, driver='evd', eigvals_only=True)
        
        # Return eigenvalues in descending order
        return eigenvalues[::-1].tolist()
---

from scipy.linalg import eigh

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        # Try the 'gvd' driver which uses a different algorithm
        eigenvalues = eigh(problem, driver='gvd', eigvals_only=True)
        
        # Return eigenvalues in descending order
        return eigenvalues[::-1].tolist()

from scipy.linalg import eigh

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        # Convert input to numpy array and compute eigenvalues using optimized driver
        eigenvalues = eigh(problem, driver='evr', eigvals_only=True)
        
        # Return eigenvalues in descending order as list
        return eigenvalues[::-1].tolist()

I apologize for the continued confusion. I'll create the solver.py file with an optimized implementation using SciPy's `eigh` function which is faster than NumPy's for symmetric matrices:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 0-0
---
import numpy as np
from scipy.linalg import eigh

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        """Compute eigenvalues of symmetric matrix using optimized SciPy implementation."""
        # Convert input to NumPy array
        A = np.array(problem)
        
        # Compute eigenvalues using SciPy's eigh (optimized for symmetric matrices)
        eigenvalues = eigh(A, eigvals_only=True)
        
        # Sort in descending order and return as list
        return sorted(eigenvalues, reverse=True)

    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        """Compute eigenvalues of symmetric matrix using optimized SciPy implementation."""
        # Use the 'evd' driver for fastest computation (divide-and-conquer)
        eigenvalues = eigh(problem, driver='evd', eigvals_only=True)
        
        # Return eigenvalues in descending order
        return eigenvalues[::-1].tolist()

Here are the commands available to you. Ensure you include one and only one of the following commands in each of your responses:
- `edit`: Replace a range of lines with new content in a file. This is how you can create files: if the file does not exist, it will be created. Here is an example:
```
edit
file: <file_name>
lines: <start_line>-<end_line>
---
<new_content>
---
```

The command will:
1. Delete the lines from <start_line> to <end_line> (inclusive)
2. Insert <new_content> starting at <start_line>
3. If both <start_line> and <end_line> are 0, <new_content> will be prepended to the file

Example:
edit
file: solver.py
lines: 5-7

- `ls`: List all files in the current working directory.
- `view_file <file_name> [start_line]`: Display 100 lines of `<file_name>` starting from `start_line` (defaults to line 1).
- `revert`: Revert the code to the best-performing version thus far.
- `reference <string>`: Query the reference solver with a problem and receive its solution. If the problem's input is a list, this command would look like:
```
reference [1,2,3,4]
```
- `eval_input <string>`: Run your current solver implementation on the given input. This is the only command that shows stdout from your solver along with both solutions. Example:
```
eval_input [1,2,3,4]
```
- `eval`: Run evaluation on the current solution and report the results.
- `delete`: Delete a range of lines from a file using the format:
```
delete
file: <file_name>
lines: <start_line>-<end_line>

The command will delete the lines from <start_line> to <end_line> (inclusive)

Example:
```
delete
file: solver.py
lines: 5-10
```
- `profile <filename.py> <input>`: Profile your currently loaded solve method's performance on a given input. Shows the 25 most time-consuming lines. Requires specifying a python file (e.g., `solver.py`) for validation, though profiling runs on the current in-memory code.
Example:
```
profile solver.py [1, 2, 3]
```
- `profile_lines <filename.py> <line_number1, line_number2, ...> <input>`: Profiles the chosen lines of the currently loaded code on the given input. Requires specifying a python file for validation.
Example:
```
profile_lines solver.py 1,2,3 [1, 2, 3]
```

import numpy as np
from scipy.linalg import eigh

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        """Compute eigenvalues of symmetric matrix using optimized SciPy implementation."""
        # Use the 'evd' driver for fastest computation (divide-and-conquer)
        eigenvalues = eigh(problem, driver='evd', eigvals_only=True)
        
        # Return eigenvalues in descending order
        return eigenvalues[::-1].tolist()

The error occurs because the 'gvd' driver is for generalized eigenvalue problems (Ax = ŒªBx) and requires two matrices. For standard eigenvalue problems (Ax = Œªx), we should use a different driver. Let's revert to the 'evr' driver which worked well previously:

        eigenvalues = eigh(problem, driver='evr', eigvals_only=True)

import numpy as np
from scipy.linalg import eigh

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        """Compute eigenvalues of symmetric matrix using optimized SciPy implementation."""
        # Use the 'evr' driver for fastest computation with check_finite=False
        eigenvalues = eigh(problem, driver='evr', check_finite=False, eigvals_only=True)
        
        # Return eigenvalues in descending order
        return eigenvalues[::-1].tolist()

        # Use the 'evr' driver for fastest computation with check_finite=False

import numpy as np
from scipy.linalg import eigh

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        """Compute eigenvalues of symmetric matrix using optimized SciPy implementation."""
        # Use the 'evr' driver for fastest computation with check_finite=False

file: solver.py

import jax.numpy as jnp
from jax.scipy.linalg import eigh

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        """Compute eigenvalues of symmetric matrix using JAX with float64 precision."""
        # Convert to JAX array with float64 precision
        jax_matrix = jnp.array(problem, dtype=jnp.float64)
        
        # Compute eigenvalues
        eigenvalues = eigh(jax_matrix, eigvals_only=True)
        
        # Return eigenvalues in descending order
        return eigenvalues[::-1].tolist()

The JAX implementation with float64 precision is still not meeting the numerical accuracy requirements. Let's revert to the best-performing version we had (the SciPy implementation with 'evr' driver) and try to optimize it further by avoiding the list conversion and using array slicing:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-14
---
import numpy as np
from scipy.linalg import eigh

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        """Compute eigenvalues of symmetric matrix using optimized SciPy implementation."""
        # Use the 'evr' driver for fastest computation with check_finite=False
        eigenvalues = eigh(problem, driver='evr', check_finite=False, eigvals_only=True)
        
        # Return eigenvalues in descending order using slicing for better performance
        return eigenvalues[::-1].tolist()

- `view_file <file_name> [start_line]`: Display 100 lines of `<file_name>` starting from `start_line` (defaults to line 1).
- `revert`: Revert the code to the best-performing version thus far.
- `reference <string>`: Query the reference solver with a problem and receive its solution. If the problem's input is a list, this command would look like:
```
reference [1,2,3,4]
```
- `eval_input <string>`: Run your current solver implementation on the given input. This is the only command that shows stdout from your solver along with both solutions. Example:
```
eval_input [1,2,3,4]
```
- `eval`: Run evaluation on the current solution and report the results.
- `delete`: Delete a range of lines from a file using the format:
```
delete
file: <file_name>
lines: <start_line>-<end_line>

The command will delete the lines from <start_line> to <end_line> (inclusive)

Example:
```
delete
file: solver.py
lines: 5-10
```
- `profile <filename.py> <input>`: Profile your currently loaded solve method's performance on a given input. Shows the 25 most time-consuming lines. Requires specifying a python file (e.g., `solver.py`) for validation, though profiling runs on the current in-memory code.
Example:
```
profile solver.py [1, 2, 3]
```
- `profile_lines <filename.py> <line_number1, line_number2, ...> <input>`: Profiles the chosen lines of the currently loaded code on the given input. Requires specifying a python file for validation.
Example:
```
profile_lines solver.py 1,2,3 [1, 2, 3]
```

import numpy as np
from scipy.linalg import eigh

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        """Compute eigenvalues of symmetric matrix using optimized SciPy implementation."""
        # Use the 'evr' driver for fastest computation with check_finite=False
        eigenvalues = eigh(problem, driver='evr', check_finite=False, eigvals_only=True)
        
        # Return eigenvalues in descending order using slicing for better performance
        return eigenvalues[::-1].tolist()

import numpy as np
from scipy.linalg import eigh

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        """Compute eigenvalues of symmetric matrix using optimized SciPy implementation."""
        # Convert input to numpy array
        A = np.array(problem, dtype=np.float64)
        
        # Use the 'evr' driver for fastest computation with check_finite=False
        eigenvalues = eigh(A, driver='evr', check_finite=False, eigvals_only=True)
        
        # Return eigenvalues in descending order using slicing for better performance
        return eigenvalues[::-1].tolist()
---

Language Model ‚úèÔ∏è Code Edit
The evaluation shows a 2.39x speedup over the reference implementation, which is good but we can still improve. Let's try a different optimization approach using the symmetric eigenvalue solver from NumPy with optimized parameters:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-14
---
import numpy as np

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        """Compute eigenvalues of symmetric matrix using optimized NumPy implementation."""
        # Convert input to numpy array with optimized data type
        A = np.asarray(problem, dtype=np.float64)
        
        # Use symmetric eigenvalue decomposition with optimized parameters
        eigenvalues = np.linalg.eigvalsh(A, UPLO='L')
        
        # Return eigenvalues in descending order
        return eigenvalues[::-1].tolist()

Language Model ‚úèÔ∏è Code Edit
The evaluation shows a 2.49x speedup, which is slightly better than the previous 2.39x. Let's try to further optimize by using a more efficient method for eigenvalue computation. The Lanczos algorithm is particularly efficient for symmetric matrices, and SciPy provides an implementation:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-13
---
import numpy as np
from scipy.sparse.linalg import eigsh

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        """Compute eigenvalues using Lanczos algorithm for symmetric matrices."""
        A = np.array(problem, dtype=np.float64)
        n = A.shape[0]
        
        # Use Lanczos method (eigsh) to compute all eigenvalues
        eigenvalues = eigsh(A, k=n, which='LM', return_eigenvectors=False)
        
        # Return in descending order
        return sorted(eigenvalues, reverse=True)
---

import numpy as np
from scipy.linalg import eigh

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        # Convert input to numpy array
        A = np.array(problem, dtype=np.float64)
        
        # Compute eigenvalues using optimized LAPACK driver
        eigenvalues = eigh(A, driver='evr', eigvals_only=True)
        
        # Sort in descending order and return as list
        return list(eigenvalues[::-1])

import numpy as np
from scipy.linalg import eigh

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        # Convert input to numpy array
        A = np.array(problem, dtype=np.float64)
        
        # Compute eigenvalues using optimized LAPACK driver
        eigenvalues = eigh(A, driver='evr', eigvals_only=True)
        
        # Sort in descending order and return as list
        return list(eigenvalues[::-1])

IMPORTANT: Each set of triple backticks (```) must always be on their own line, without any other words or anything else on that line.

Here are the commands available to you. Ensure you include one and only one of the following commands in each of your responses:
- `edit`: Replace a range of lines with new content in a file. This is how you can create files: if the file does not exist, it will be created. Here is an example:
```
edit
file: <file_name>
lines: <start_line>-<end_line>
---
<new_content>
---
```

The command will:
1. Delete the lines from <start_line> to <end_line> (inclusive)
2. Insert <new_content> starting at <start_line>
3. If both <start_line> and <end_line> are 0, <new_content> will be prepended to the file

Example:
edit
file: solver.py
lines: 5-7
---
def improved_function():
print("Optimized solution")

- `ls`: List all files in the current working directory.
- `view_file <file_name> [start_line]`: Display 100 lines of `<file_name>` starting from `start_line` (defaults to line 1).
- `revert`: Revert the code to the best-performing version thus far.
- `reference <string>`: Query the reference solver with a problem and receive its solution. If the problem's input is a list, this command would look like:
```
reference [1,2,3,4]
```
- `eval_input <string>`: Run your current solver implementation on the given input. This is the only command that shows stdout from your solver along with both solutions. Example:
```
eval_input [1,2,3,4]
```
- `eval`: Run evaluation on the current solution and report the results.
- `delete`: Delete a range of lines from a file using the format:
```
delete
file: <file_name>
lines: <start_line>-<end_line>

The command will delete the lines from <start_line> to <end_line> (inclusive)

Example:
```

file: solver.py
lines: 5-10
```
- `profile <filename.py> <input>`: Profile your currently loaded solve method's performance on a given input. Shows the 25 most time-consuming lines. Requires specifying a python file (e.g., `solver.py`) for validation, though profiling runs on the current in-memory code.
Example:
```
profile solver.py [1, 2, 3]
```
- `profile_lines <filename.py> <line_number1, line_number2, ...> <input>`: Profiles the chosen lines of the currently loaded code on the given input. Requires specifying a python file for validation.
Example:
```

import numpy as np
from scipy.linalg import eigh

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        # Convert input to numpy array
        A = np.array(problem, dtype=np.float64)
        
        # Compute eigenvalues using optimized LAPACK driver
        eigenvalues = eigh(A, driver='evr', eigvals_only=True)
        
        # Sort in descending order and return as list
        return eigenvalues[::-1].tolist()

reference [1,2,3,4]
```
- `eval_input <string>`: Run your current solver implementation on the given input. This is the only command that shows stdout from your solver along with both solutions. Example:
```
eval_input [1,2,3,4]
```
- `eval`: Run evaluation on the current solution and report the results.
- `delete`: Delete a range of lines from a file using the format:
```
delete
file: <file_name>
lines: <start_line>-<end_line>

The command will delete the lines from <start_line> to <end_line> (inclusive)

Example:
```
delete
file: solver.py
lines: 5-10
```
- `profile <filename.py> <input>`: Profile your currently loaded solve method's performance on a given input. Shows the 25 most time-consuming lines. Requires specifying a python file (e.g., `solver.py`) for validation, though profiling runs on the current in-memory code.
Example:
```
profile solver.py [1, 2, 3]
```
- `profile_lines <filename.py> <line_number1, line_number2, ...> <input>`: Profiles the chosen lines of the currently loaded code on the given input. Requires specifying a python file for validation.
Example:
```
profile_lines solver.py 1,2,3 [1, 2, 3]
```

---
import numpy as np
from scipy.linalg import eigh

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        # Convert input to numpy array with optimal dtype
        A = np.array(problem, dtype=np.float64)
        
        # Compute eigenvalues using optimized LAPACK driver
        # 'evr' is the fastest driver for symmetric matrices
        # Added overwrite_a and check_finite to optimize performance
        eigenvalues = eigh(A, driver='evr', eigvals_only=True, overwrite_a=True, check_finite=False)
        
        # Return eigenvalues in descending order
        return eigenvalues[::-1].tolist()

import numpy as np
from scipy.linalg import eigh

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        # Convert input to numpy array with optimal dtype
        A = np.array(problem, dtype=np.float64)
        
        # Compute eigenvalues using optimized LAPACK driver
        # 'evr' is the fastest driver for symmetric matrices
        # Added overwrite_a and check_finite to optimize performance
        eigenvalues = eigh(A, driver='evr', eigvals_only=True, overwrite_a=True, check_finite=False)
        
        # Return eigenvalues in descending order
        return eigenvalues[::-1].tolist()

```
reference [1,2,3,4]
```

```

```

- `revert`: Revert the code to the best-performing version thus far.
- `reference <string>`: Query the reference solver with a problem and receive its solution. If the problem's input is a list, this command would look like:
```
reference [1,2,3,4]
```
- `eval_input <string>`: Run your current solver implementation on the given input. This is the only command that shows stdout from your solver along with both solutions. Example:
```
eval_input [1,2,3,4]
```
- `eval`: Run evaluation on the current solution and report the results.
- `delete`: Delete a range of lines from a file using the format:
```
delete
file: <file_name>
lines: <start_line>-<end_line>

The command will delete the lines from <start_line> to <end_line> (inclusive)

Example:
```
delete
file: solver.py
lines: 5-10
```
- `profile <filename.py> <input>`: Profile your currently loaded solve method's performance on a given input. Shows the 25 most time-consuming lines. Requires specifying a python file (e.g., `solver.py`) for validation, though profiling runs on the current in-memory code.
Example:
```
profile solver.py [1, 2, 3]
```
- `profile_lines <filename.py> <line_number1, line_number2, ...> <input>`: Profiles the chosen lines of the currently loaded code on the given input. Requires specifying a python file for validation.
Example:
```
profile_lines solver.py 1,2,3 [1, 2, 3]
```

import numpy as np
from scipy.linalg import eigh

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        # Convert input to NumPy array
        A = np.array(problem, dtype=np.float64)
        
        # Compute eigenvalues using optimized LAPACK driver
        eigenvalues = eigh(A, eigvals_only=True, overwrite_a=True, check_finite=False)
        
        # Sort in descending order and return
        return sorted(eigenvalues, reverse=True)

import numpy as np
from scipy.linalg import eigh

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        # Convert input to NumPy array
        A = np.array(problem, dtype=np.float64)
        
        # Compute eigenvalues using optimized LAPACK driver
        eigenvalues = eigh(A, driver='evd', eigvals_only=True, overwrite_a=True, check_finite=False)
        
        # Return eigenvalues in descending order
        return eigenvalues[::-1].tolist()

The 'evd' driver provided a slight speedup improvement to 2.36x. Let's try the 'evr' driver which gave us the best results earlier:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-13
---
import numpy as np
from scipy.linalg import eigh

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        # Convert input to NumPy array
        A = np.array(problem, dtype=np.float64)
        
        # Compute eigenvalues using optimized LAPACK driver
        eigenvalues = eigh(A, driver='evr', eigvals_only=True, overwrite_a=True, check_finite=False)
        
        # Return eigenvalues in descending order
        return eigenvalues[::-1].tolist()
---
